<div id="review_721156238" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/721156238" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72837-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178153459p2/72837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/721156238?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/72837-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer721156238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11856998451689123731">Assigned this book for a class on Freud's Uncanny and Horror Literature, and had a tough time presenting James in such a way that the students got beyond his "reasonable" facade and into deeper, weirder territory. Yes, James is a master of "understatement" - although I wonder if this isn't just a function of the narrative layers and frames he's so fond of employing - but he uses this light touch to investigate some deeper strain of desire. All of these academics, desperate to uncover previously </span>
  <span id="freeText11856998451689123731" style="display:none">Assigned this book for a class on Freud's Uncanny and Horror Literature, and had a tough time presenting James in such a way that the students got beyond his "reasonable" facade and into deeper, weirder territory. Yes, James is a master of "understatement" - although I wonder if this isn't just a function of the narrative layers and frames he's so fond of employing - but he uses this light touch to investigate some deeper strain of desire. All of these academics, desperate to uncover previously buried secrets, get exactly what they deserve in the form of demons, "hairy hopping things," a whole array of (unwanted?) physical touch from loathsome, tentacled members. In other words, the deeper one investigates James' unconscious territory the more interesting the stories become. I particularly enjoyed "Count Magnus," which so enthralled H.P. Lovecraft, both in its calm deployment of a frame and the eventual destruction of the frame by the desire for access to history, as if the characters are desperate to reach through a picture and inhabit it, only to have the figures in the painting reach out to them. <br />Adventures at the edge of "forbidden" knowledge, frustrated desire, and the appearance of the shadowy demon double. No better arena for the dissolution of the bourgeois subject than horror literature, amiright? Loved this book. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11856998451689123731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Riju Ganguly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2736147-riju-ganguly"><img alt="Riju Ganguly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524633338p2/2736147.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/222335655?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Riju Ganguly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Riju Ganguly" href="/user/show/2736147-riju-ganguly">Riju Ganguly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780199568840" href="/book/show/11325318-collected-ghost-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222335655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12807275080979049955">There are classics that deserve no separate review apart from mere stating of the fact that these stories have been shaping the contours of horror fiction for the past century, and since 1931 (when these stories were brought out together, except 3 stories that were later collected) they have remained continuously in-print. However, this book was special in the sense that the stories often mention certain details that require gentle ministrations in the shape of annotations & explanations for Lat</span>
  <span id="freeText12807275080979049955" style="display:none">There are classics that deserve no separate review apart from mere stating of the fact that these stories have been shaping the contours of horror fiction for the past century, and since 1931 (when these stories were brought out together, except 3 stories that were later collected) they have remained continuously in-print. However, this book was special in the sense that the stories often mention certain details that require gentle ministrations in the shape of annotations &amp; explanations for Latin-challenged readers like us (who must be 99.9% of the total readers), and this book does it to some extent. Of course, its comparison with the gold-standard of annotated editions set by "A Pleasing Terror" published by the Ash Tree Press would be made, and the book would be found short on many grounds (esp. in terms of non-inclusion of certain non-fiction written by MRJ himself that have immense relevance in understanding the stories themselves) rather inevitably. Nevertheless, it is very reasonably priced, and deserves to be appreciated, esp. since the Penguin volumes (edited by S.T Joshi) are ridiculously overpriced, and since the Ash Tree Press is not coming out with their long-awaited 2nd edition of "A Pleasing Terror" (in 2 volumes!) any time soon. Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12807275080979049955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17268151875389319158">A timeless collection of England’s greatest ever ghost story writer - MR James.<br/>Often read at Christmas but great to read at anytime of the year, this collection of short often very unsettling ghost stories are easy to read and digest quickly but leave an indelible impression in your mind and imagination.<br/>There are too many to mention here but my three personal favourites are The Ash Tree, The Tractate Middoth and one of MR James most famous ghost stories, Oh Whistle and I’ll come to you my Lad.<br/>Cl</span>
  <span id="freeText17268151875389319158" style="display:none">A timeless collection of England’s greatest ever ghost story writer - MR James.<br />Often read at Christmas but great to read at anytime of the year, this collection of short often very unsettling ghost stories are easy to read and digest quickly but leave an indelible impression in your mind and imagination.<br />There are too many to mention here but my three personal favourites are The Ash Tree, The Tractate Middoth and one of MR James most famous ghost stories, Oh Whistle and I’ll come to you my Lad.<br />Classic, old but still as riveting and addictive as they must have been when first published, these are scary stories of classic ‘bump in the night’ phantoms and unsettling happenings which really stay with you long after finishing. Definitely a book to be read late at night with a candle lit and the windows, doors and curtains firmly closed.......</span>

<div id="review_3627378099" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3627378099" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51196399-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597783321p2/51196399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3627378099?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/51196399-gary">Gary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780713152340" href="/book/show/7108092-the-collected-ghost-stories-of-m-r-james">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3627378099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6400786967018942627">Set from around the 1880s to 1early 20th century, these ghost stories are more subtle in the horror and more polished in the scene set, and the detective work involved in solving the mystery.<br/>Many of them were written to be told as Christmas Eve enterntainments read to gatherings of friends.<br/>The horror usually comes at the end and precisely because of the scene set is what makes the stories memorable in their own way.<br/>Set usually in a country or seaside town, or an abbey or old university or librar</span>
  <span id="freeText6400786967018942627" style="display:none">Set from around the 1880s to 1early 20th century, these ghost stories are more subtle in the horror and more polished in the scene set, and the detective work involved in solving the mystery.<br />Many of them were written to be told as Christmas Eve enterntainments read to gatherings of friends.<br />The horror usually comes at the end and precisely because of the scene set is what makes the stories memorable in their own way.<br />Set usually in a country or seaside town, or an abbey or old university or library, often in England, but sometimes in Scandinavia or France, the stories usually involve a rather dull, rather naive scholar-gentleman who picks up some item ( a book or painting etc) such as in Canon's Scrap Book, The Mezzotint and Number 13, which awakens angry and malevolent spirits.<br />Or else the narrator-protagonist tells of a story he has heard from a secondary source in a place where he is staying weather on work or vacation such as Lost Hearts or Martin's Close.<br />Sometimes the horror of the discovery of the supernatural being is enough to chill the reader, sometimes the spirits involved, as in the Ash-Tree or Lost Hearts result in the death of the victim of their revenge.<br />An interesting read which requires some concentration not to lose the plot, good reading for the lover of older supernatural fiction.<br />Always told in a scholarly and carefully descriptive way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6400786967018942627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1417804674" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1417804674" />
    <a title="Benjamin Stahl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16968146-benjamin-stahl"><img alt="Benjamin Stahl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592923113p2/16968146.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1417804674?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Stahl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Stahl" href="/user/show/16968146-benjamin-stahl">Benjamin Stahl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/394836.Collected_Ghost_Stories">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1417804674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15966623870701939315">I was introduced to M.R. James after reading the <i>Wordsworth Book Of Horror Stories</i>; a 1000-plus page compilation of all the greatest horror tales written between the eighteenth and twentieth century. It contained many obscure treasures by authors all over England, Europe and America. I recall there being an Aussie in there as well. Of course, the book was slow going at times. But overall, I found it thrillingly scary and enchanting to read. It single-handedly started my love, not just for pre-19</span>
  <span id="freeText15966623870701939315" style="display:none">I was introduced to M.R. James after reading the <i>Wordsworth Book Of Horror Stories</i>; a 1000-plus page compilation of all the greatest horror tales written between the eighteenth and twentieth century. It contained many obscure treasures by authors all over England, Europe and America. I recall there being an Aussie in there as well. Of course, the book was slow going at times. But overall, I found it thrillingly scary and enchanting to read. It single-handedly started my love, not just for pre-1900 horror fiction, but classic literature in general. I have never forgiven myself for giving the book away, since then, to some charity shop or perhaps my church, so that some other lucky schmuck can get hold of it. If anyone has this on their shelves, or ever comes across it in a second-hand store, I would highly recommend buying it. It’s easily worth $40 at least, and yet I got it new for $20. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1446205909i/16765059.jpg" width='“500"' height='“200"' alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12927558-the-wordsworth-book-of-horror-stories">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a><br><br>Among many other literary greats, like Doyle, Dickens, Le Fanu, and Ball-sack, this book featured an inconsistent concentration of James’s work. But all the better for it. I remember being mesmerised with almost every one of his stories. That was back in 2010 or somewhere thereof, and apart from reading <i>Oh, Whistle</i> once more in another compilation, I haven’t come across any of James’s stuff since. That is, not until this book, which I was more than excited about starting. Revisiting another favourite classic - (that being Bram Stoker’s <i>Dracula</i>) - the experience could not have been more rewarding, and I was certain that I would love this guy’s short but chilling work just as much, if not <i>more</i>, on reading it again. <br><br>Unfortunately, things didn’t turn out quite that way. And it still hurts and baffles me to say this. But I don’t mean any disrespect to him, because this book was, by all means, very enjoyable, and often times scary. It just didn’t live up to the greatness that I have, until now, attributed to his work. I understand that many still idolise him, and I suppose I still do, as well. It’s just that I don’t think I would call him one of my favourites anymore. The point is, on revisiting what pretty much makes up the entirety his work, I found myself enjoying it little more than I did <i>similar</i> compilations for the likes of Rudyard Kipling, Robert Louis Stevenson, and Wilkie Collins. There were a number of really great stories, but overall, I think that most of them started to seem a little repetitive and not altogether satisfying in the end.<br><br>Firstly, I’ll just list my favourite ones, which I had little to hold against. These were all genuinely creepy, or at least interesting. <i>Lost Hearts</i>; which I’m sure I hadn’t read before, and this one was particularly scary; (I also liked the black humour in the story’s title). <i>The Ash Tree</i> was average at first, but the ending stayed with me a night or two. <i>Number 13</i>, again, was actually pretty average, but for a few little touches which made it hard to shake off; (like, for instance, the image of that door suddenly opening slightly, and the arm reaching out and clawing at the air). <i>Oh, Whistle, And I’ll Come To You, My Lad</i>, I always considered to be James’s equivalent to Bierce’s <i>An Occurrence At Owl Creek Bridge</i>, in that it was certainly decent, but not deserving as being his most famous story, as he had <i>several</i> superior ones out there. Well, after reading this a third time - (and alongside all his other stories) - I think I’ll comply with the majority now. This probably <i>is</i> his best story. <i>The Tractate Middoth</i> and <i>Casting The Runes</i> were similar in that neither was all that scary, but I certainly got involved in the character’s plight. And then there was <i>An Episode Of Cathedral History</i>, which was actually another pretty frightening story. <br><br>These stories were all great. And, to be fair, there weren’t any <i>bad</i> stories in this book. But there were many that just seemed too average to be given much thought or credit. Certain tropes of James became a little noticeable after reading all his stuff together. I found it kind of annoying, how almost all his stories had that “<i>It happened to a friend of a friend of a colleague of mine</i>” structure to them. Also, James was often very good at building suspense and setting his atmosphere perfectly ... only to break the subtlety by bringing some lame hairy monster into the equation. (Many of his stories’ monsters were described as being covered in grey hairs, and wearing dark cloaks). It was fine the first couple of times, but after a while it got a little irritating. <br>I also found - (and I could be overstepping here, holding my own personal failings against him) - but it did seem to me that his descriptions of geographical landscapes was a little dry and uninspired. I found myself having to pause and strain my mind to picture his locales more clearly, because he didn’t give me much, and what he <i>did</i> give me - (things like architectural features at churches and mansion exteriors) - I just didn’t understand the anatomies for in the first place, and so found myself picturing, for most of these stories, backdrops no less vague than that presented in a high-school play. <br><br>None of these are exactly major flaws, but they did get in the way of me getting as much enjoyment out of the book as I might have done. But all in all, I still can’t bring myself to saying anything of real meaning against M.R. James. I still admire the man, and I still love that period of horror fiction. I just think that, in retrospect, reading the book from start to finish might not have been the best idea. Just like Edgar Allen Poe, his greatness is more easily appreciated when consumed in smaller doses. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15966623870701939315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_990662389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/990662389" />
    <a title="Geoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21465898-geoff"><img alt="Geoff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458073995p2/21465898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/990662389?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoff" href="/user/show/21465898-geoff">Geoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21465898-geoff?shelf=horror-suspense">horror-suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21465898-geoff?shelf=just-plain-weird">just-plain-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21465898-geoff?shelf=favs">favs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21465898-geoff?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21465898-geoff?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21465898-geoff?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer990662389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3330836777581650490">M.R. James is one of those kinda guys you can just picture at a bar, telling the wildest story imaginable, complete with "It was thiiis big" alongside arm gestures and hyperboles in excess. Although what's funny, is how un-hyperbole-ish his stories actually are. In fact they're understated almost, quiet and full of nerdy guys wandering around ancient ruins and museums and churches strolling along as innocents, until things get crazy. Similar to Lovecraft, and certainly in what I would call the "</span>
  <span id="freeText3330836777581650490" style="display:none">M.R. James is one of those kinda guys you can just picture at a bar, telling the wildest story imaginable, complete with "It was thiiis big" alongside arm gestures and hyperboles in excess. Although what's funny, is how un-hyperbole-ish his stories actually are. In fact they're understated almost, quiet and full of nerdy guys wandering around ancient ruins and museums and churches strolling along as innocents, until things get crazy. Similar to Lovecraft, and certainly in what I would call the "Holy Quadrinity of Horror Literature," right next to Lovecraft, Poe, and King, his stories almost have a legendary quality about them. Highly atmospheric, they FEEL like urban myths or dusty old tales that have been hidden throughout the English countryside for eons, waiting for some studious professor to find them in a barn or an attic and write them onto paper. There's just that solidly eerie feel to it that makes him so good. Like you almost wonder if some of these actually did happen, and were relayed to the narrator. And the nerdy Oxford gent feel solidifies that. The way the stories are told are intense, creeping, and full of passion. When the story begins, you begin with the quaint professor. When it ends you fall into madness with him. And its the focus on the heebie jeebies and not the monsters themselves that make the stories so good. Its almost like James uses mirrors, smoke and shadows and fleeting objects in the corner of your eye to accomplish that feel. That true heebie jeebie feel. Its what makes him a true master. Ever wonder why ghosts are sheets? Or why howls in forests at midnight have become staples of horror films and fiction? Just a few examples of how timeless and iconic his works are. Fully deserving of five freakywhackedoutinsane stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3330836777581650490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2176954017" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2176954017" />
    <a title="BellaGBear" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14546476-bellagbear"><img alt="BellaGBear" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390168030p2/14546476.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2176954017?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BellaGBear" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BellaGBear" href="/user/show/14546476-bellagbear">BellaGBear</a>
      </span>

          marked it as to-read




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14546476-bellagbear?shelf=bookworms-united">bookworms-united</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2176954017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4997112273201188441">Because there are altogether thirty-one stories in this collection, all with different characters and playing at different times, I can’t give you a quick introduction to what it’s all about. What I can do, is tell you the premise of one of the stories that I thought was the scariest: ‘Lost Hearts’.<br/><br/>An eleven year old boy, recently orphaned, arrives at a country house in the year 1811. He is to live with his rich old cousin, who is a learned recluse, specialised in pagan religions from the first </span>
  <span id="freeText4997112273201188441" style="display:none">Because there are altogether thirty-one stories in this collection, all with different characters and playing at different times, I can’t give you a quick introduction to what it’s all about. What I can do, is tell you the premise of one of the stories that I thought was the scariest: ‘Lost Hearts’.<br /><br />An eleven year old boy, recently orphaned, arrives at a country house in the year 1811. He is to live with his rich old cousin, who is a learned recluse, specialised in pagan religions from the first centuries AD. The man isn’t sociable, but by all accounts a kind guardian who has taken in abandoned children before. The boy has been living in the big house for a few months, in the care of the housekeeper, when this kind lady scolds him for having torn his nightdress. The boy doesn’t know anything about the rips in the cloth. “But,” he said, “Mrs Bunch, they are just the same as the scratches on the outside of my bedroom door; and I’m sure I never had anything to do with making them.” Things escalate from that point.<br /><br />Of course, I did not like all the stories equally. Some of the evil creatures, most of them hooded and operating in the shadows, were quite unpleasant, but I noticed that I found the stories containing actual, physical monsters less scary than those with some kind of ghost or witchcraft. The build-up to the revealing of the monster was usually scarier than the thing itself. Isn’t that always the case?<br /><br /><i> Reviewed by the lovely Jo for the Bookworms United bookblog. See the full review here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://bookwormsshallruletheworld.wordpress.com/2017/11/07/ghost-stories-by-m-r-james/">https://bookwormsshallruletheworld.wo...</a> </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4997112273201188441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4dRz4+N8MOIso26cCTQoVGvRsjXJR367liHDib7GwOFPPg3yxyVH3O+MynavZIxtIjY61U/suhRDpgxbzUhl7Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_36651542'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36651542-the-great-god-pan-and-other-horror-stories?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Great God Pan and Other Horror Stories<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/33546.Arthur_Machen?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Arthur Machen<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.89 avg rating &mdash; 277 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2018\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer14654147768932923907\">Something pushed out from the body there on the floor, and stretched forth a slimy, wavering tentacle... Perhaps no figure better embodies the transition from the Gothic tradition to modern horror than Arthur Machen. In the final decade of the ninete<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14654147768932923907\" style=\"display:none\">Something pushed out from the body there on the floor, and stretched forth a slimy, wavering tentacle... Perhaps no figure better embodies the transition from the Gothic tradition to modern horror than Arthur Machen. In the final decade of the nineteenth century, the Welsh writer produced a seminal body of tales of occult horror, spiritual and physical corruption, and malignant survivals from the primeval past which horrified and scandalized late-Victorian readers. Machen\'s &quot;weird fiction&quot; has influenced generations of storytellers, from H. P. Lovecraft to Guillermo Del Toro - and it remains no less unsettling today. This new collection, which includes the complete novel The Three Impostors as well as such celebrated tales as The Great God Pan and The White People, constitutes the most comprehensive critical edition of Machen yet to appear. In addition to the core late-Victorian horror classics, a selection of lesser-known prose poems and later tales helps to present a fuller picture of the development of Machen\'s weird vision. The edition\'s introduction and notes contextualize the life and work of this foundational figure in the history of horror.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14654147768932923907\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' id=\'8_book_36651542\'>\n<div class=\'wtrUp wtrLeft\'>\n<form Altogether 13 stories, gathered from the entire corpus of Blackwood\'s work, are included; stories of such sheer power and imagination that it is easy to see why he has been considered the foremost British supernaturalist of the twentieth century.\n\nBlackwood\'s ability to create an atmosphere of unrelieved horror and sustain it to the end of the story is almost unsurpassed. “The Willows” — which has been called by H.P. Lovecraft the finest supernatural story — is a typical example of Blackwood\'s art: slowly and surely Blackwood draws the reader into a world of shadows, nuances and unearthly terror.\n\nBlackwood was also a master at evoking feelings of mysticism and cosmic experience; dealing with such ideas as interpenetrating levels of existence and pantheistic elemental powers, he expanded the content of supernatural literature enormously. But even the more traditional elements of horror stories such as ghosts and haunted houses are handled with such energy and feeling that they rise far above their predecessors.\n\nDrawing on serious Oriental thought, modern psychology and philosophy, Algernon Blackwood introduced a sophistication to the horror story that — with a few exceptions — it was devoid of before. The results are stories that are not only guaranteed to chill, but stories that have something to say to the intelligent reader.\n\nContents:\n- The Willows (1907)\n- Secret Worship (1908)\n- Ancient Sorceries (1908)\n- The Glamour of the Snow (1911)\n- The Wendigo (1910)\n- The Other Wing (1915)\n- The Transfer (1911)\n- Ancient Lights (1914)\n- The Listener (1907)\n- The Empty House (1906)\n- The story Pages from a Young Girl\'s Journal won Aickman the World Fantasy Award in 1975. It was originally published in The Magazine of Fantasy &amp; Science Fiction in 1973 before appearing in this collection.\n\nCold Hand in Mine stands as one of Aickman\'s best collections and contains eight stories that show off his powers as a \'strange story\' writer to the full, being more ambiguous than standard ghost stories. Throughout the stories the reader is introduced to a variety of characters, from a man who spends the night in a Hospice to a German aristocrat and a woman who sees an image of her own soul. There is also a nod to the conventional vampire story (Pages from a Young Girl\'s Journal) but all the stories remain unconventional and inconclusive, which perhaps makes them all the more startling O<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6664449937526624510\" style=\"display:none\">The Great God Pan&quot; is a novella written by Arthur Machen. A version of the story was published in the magazine Whirlwind in 1890, and Machen revised and extended it for its book publication (together with another story, &quot;The Inmost Light&quot;) in 1894. On publication it was widely denounced by the press as degenerate and horrific because of its decadent style and sexual content, although it has since garnered a reputation as a classic of horror.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2995925.M_R_James">About M.R. James</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2995925.M_R_James"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1254798756p3/2995925.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2995925.M_R_James">M.R. James
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
623
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7990108613429497747">Montague Rhodes James, who used the publication name M.R. James, was a noted British mediaeval scholar & provost of King's College, Cambridge (1905–18) & of Eton College (1918–36). He's best remembered for his ghost stories which are widely regarded as among the finest in English literature. One of James' most important achievements was to redefine the ghost story for the new century by dispensing</span>
  <span id="freeText7990108613429497747" style="display:none">Montague Rhodes James, who used the publication name M.R. James, was a noted British mediaeval scholar &amp; provost of King's College, Cambridge (1905–18) &amp; of Eton College (1918–36). He's best remembered for his ghost stories which are widely regarded as among the finest in English literature. One of James' most important achievements was to redefine the ghost story for the new century by dispensing with many of the formal Gothic trappings of his predecessors, replacing them with more realistic contemporary settings.<br /><br /><i>Librarian note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /><br />M.R.^James</span>
  <a data-text-id="7990108613429497747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2083711-the-collected-ghost-stories">Quotes from Collected Ghost S...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Those that spend the greater part of their time in reading or writing books are, of course, apt to take rather particular notice of accumulations of books when they come across them. They will not pass a stall, a shop, or even a bedroom-shelf without reading some title, and if they find themselves in an unfamiliar library, no host need trouble himself further about their entertainment. The putting of dispersed sets of volumes together, or the turning right way up of those which the dusting housemaid has left in an apoplectic condition, appeals to them as one of the lesser Works of Mercy.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1196679-those-that-spend-the-greater-part-of-their-time-in">9 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The door was opening again. The seer does not like to dwell upon what he saw <br />entering the room: he says it might be described as a frog - the size of a man - but it had scanty white hair about its head. It was busy about the truckle-beds, but not for long. The sound of cries - faint, as if coming out of a vast distance - but, even so, infinitely appalling, reached the ear. ("The Haunted Doll's House")&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/529492-the-door-was-opening-again-the-seer-does-not-like">4 likes</a>
            </nobr>
